CENSUS
OF THE
MALTESE ISLANDS
REPORT.




REPORT on the Census of the Maltese Islands taken at midnight on
26th April, 1931.
PART I—INTRODUCTION.
The eleventh Census of the population of the Maltese Islands was
taken on 26th April, 1931. This report gives an account of its working,
and comments on the recorded results.
The first step in post-censal activities at the Census Office was the
preparation of a Preliminary Report. The tables comprised in this report
consisted of figures, by sexes, of (a) the total population enumerated;
(b) the civilian population enumerated at each Census between 1828 and
1931. showing the intercensal increase; and (c) the civilian population
enumerated in each district, showing the variation since the preceding
intercensal period.
The rapid compilation and publication of these tables had been
rendered possible by so arranging the work of the abstractors as to permit
of the simple figures being abstracted from the Schedules and summaries
compiled, and although the figures obtained were provisional in character,
no discrepancy manifested itself in the subsequent statistical operations
at the Census Office.
The Preliminary Report was ready on the 2nd of June, 1931, just
over five weeks from Census Day, and was published on 12th June
(Supplement No. L) in the Government Gazette No. 7445.
The special procedure adopted for the purpose of the Preliminary
Report could not, however, be applied to the main contents of the Census
returns, each entry in which had to be separately examined and classified
before the information could be expressed in statistical form, and
notwithstanding that the subsequent abstracting and tabulating processes
involved were carried out smoothly and expeditiously, the magnitude of
the work would not permit of the publication of the general report on the
Census until the lapse of several months.
This being so, a detailed account of the preparations for the Census,
of the contents of the Schedule, and of the actual course of its
administration, was given in the Preliminary Report, whilst the events
were comparatively recent. Also a very complete account of the earlier
Censuses, briefly touched upon in the Preliminary Report, is given in the
report of the Census of 1911, and it is therefore not considered necessary
on this occasion to repeat this retrospection.
There is no permanent Census Staff, and officers of the Civil Service
are employed, after the enumeration, to deal with the examination of the
enumeration registers, the revision of the schedules, and the tabulation
of the raw material obtained on Census Schedules and of the presentation
of the results. This Staff numbered: two Tabulators, seven Abstractors,
and seven Assistant Abstractors. A temporary clerk and typist performed
the ordinary clerical routine work, whilst one messenger and one
fatiguernan were also employed.
The total expenditure under the Census Vote amounted to £1,900.
This sum is inclusive of the rent of the Census Office and of the cost of
stationery, maps, etc., but exclusive of the cost of printing. The
corresponding expenditure in 1921 was £2,026, and that in 1911 £1,084.
The large increase in 1921 and 1931 is due entirely to the greatly enhanced
cost of living, which at the date of the 1921 Census was almost at its
highest. Of the abovementioned sum of £1,900, £1,074 represented the

instead of every ten as heretofore.
PART IT—POPULATION.
1. General.
It will be well before referring to the statistical record of the Census
to define precisely what is meant by population. The population consists
of all persons who were enumerated as being alive and present within the
Maltese Islands at midnight, 26th-27th April, 1931, persons on board
vessels wdiich were in port on Census night or which reached port on the
following morning being regarded as within the scope of the definition.
It comprises visitors as well as residents, persons of alien nationalities,
civilians and non-civilians; and in Tables I, 11, 111 and VI the totals
refer the aggregate population so described. All other Tables, unless
specially defined, deal with the civilian population alone.
The total population enumerated in Malta and Gozo (which includes
Commo), on the night of the 26th April, 1931, numbered 258,400 persons,
of which 134,202 were males and 124,198 females. There was an increase
in the total population of 33,720, compared with the Census of 1921,
made up of 29,363 in the “civilian” population, and of 4,357 in the
non-civilian” population, and the variations are analysed below:
In Table I the several elements of which the population is composed
are given; Table II presents the population enumerated on Land and in
Harbour; and Table 111 shows the enumeration on Land and on Vessels in
Harbour of the separate elements of the population. In Table IV the figures
for the civilian population are shown in continuation of the series of

corresponding figures of past Censuses; Table V shows the distribution of
the non-civilian population on Land, by Places, and in Harbour; and Table
VI shows the population, area and density of population to a square mile.
In this Table the non-civilian element living on land and shown in Table V
is incorporated for statistical purposes. Table VII shows by places the
civilian population of Malta and Gozo on the Census day in 1921 and 1931,
giving therein the numerical and centesimal increase during the decade.
The total civilian population is the largest ever recorded and exceeds
the number returned at the previous enumeration of 24th April, 1921, by
29,363, corresponding to an intercensal rate of increase of 13.83 per cent.,
as against 10.18 per cent, in 1891, 11.93 per cent, in 1901, 14.52 per cent,
in 1911, and 0.33 per cent, in 1921. The increase itself is also numerically
the largest in any intercensal period, while proportionately it is 0.69 per
cent, lower than that recorded in 1911.
Obviously no comparison can be made between the figures of 1921 and
1931, for owing to the upheaval caused by the war, and the unsettled period
which followed, resulting in the subsequent exodus of thousands of skilled
workmen to the United States of America, the increase in the population in
the intercensal period 1911-1921 was the lowest ever recorded, viz : 694, or
0.33 per cent. Conditions have now come down to normal and the inter-
censal increase for the period 1921-1931 is again actually on a level with
that recorded in the intercensal period 1901-1911.
As in previous Censuses, the officers and men of local military corps,
the Maltese ratings on 11. M. Ships in Harbour, and the families of officers
and men of the Royal Navy and of the Royal Air Force, have been
included with the civilian population. Wives and children of Army
officers and men, included under “Garrison” in recent Censuses, are for
the first time grouped with the civilian population. Thus under the
classification “non-civilian” population (Tables I and III) is shown solely
the personnel of the Royal Navy, of the Army and of the Royal Air Force,
viz : Naval, Military and Air Force serving officers and other ranks or
ratings and “enrolled women,” who have been enumerated in the Census
of England and Wales held on the 26th April, 1931, a Census being
simultaneously taken of Naval, Military and Air Force establishments
abroad.
The estimated civilian population of the Maltese Islands is given
by the Public Health Department as 234,454 on 31st December, 1930, and
may be taken to have been approximately, 235,260 on the 26th of April,
1931, (Census Day). This estimate based on the returns of births and
deaths and of arrivals and departures of passengers, is 6,361 short of the
actual number of persons enumerated, viz : 241,621, and the Public Health
statistical figures will no doubt be revised in the light of the published
Census figures.
The civilian element of the population is classified in the following
table according to nationality:
Of Malta’s foreign population, 59.81 per cent, were born in Italy, and
3.27 per cent, in Lybia; 6.01 per cent, in Germany, 4.75 per cent, in
France, 4.22 per cent, in the U.S.A., 4.01 per cent, in Turkey, 3.58 per
cent, in Greece, 3.38 per cent, in Hungary, and 10.97 per cent, in other
Countries.

2. Density.
The area of the Maltese Islands, according to the survey of 1921, is
121.844 square miles, or 77,980 acres, viz:
Malta 94.870 Sq. miles or 60,717 acres
G° zo _ ... ... ... 25.899 Sq, miles or 16,575 acres
Comino ... 1.075 Sq. miles or 688 acres
The density, assuming an even distribution, of the 1931 population
represents .317 acres per person or conversely 3.150 and 2,016 persons
per acre and per square mile respectively. For each Island of the Maltese
group the density, also assuming an even distribution in each of the three
Islands, is as follows;
The actual densities at each of the past Censuses from 1881 are given
in the following table:
For purposes of density the civilian population of 241,618 enumerated
on land (Table II) and the “non-civilian” population of 4,020 enumerated
on land are This total population amounts to 245,638. Table VI
shows, in detail, the density of the total population enumerated on land.
The density of the population in each town and village is shown therein.
The difference suggested by these figures is very wide, ranging from 102,053
persons per square mile in Senglea to 257 persons per square mile in
Zebbug, Gozo.
The densities per square mile in the urban, suburban and rural areas
are shown below in juxtaposition to the figures for 1921:

The density of the urban districts, therefore, increased during the
intercensal period by 1,392 persons per square mile; of the suburban districts
by 1,151 persons per square mile; and of the rural districts by 106 persons
per square mile.
The highest density in the urban group is recorded in Senglea with
102,053 persons per square mile, and the lowest in Floriana with 19,411
persons per square mile. In the suburban group it was highest in Hamrun
with 33,226, and lowest in Zabbar with 2,477 persons per square mile.
In the rural group the density was highest in Lia with 7,910, and lowest
in Mgiar with 272 persons per square mile.
In Gozo, Victoria showed the highest density with 2,798 persons per
square mile and Zebbug the lowest with 257. ‘ r

These figures, of course, represent the average population per square
mile in determinate areas classified as urban, suburban and rural. But
it must be remembered that densities in this composite form are of little
interest as real guides to population pressure. As a rule populations are
massed together in well defined areas and the density figures, therefore,
for suburban and rural areas, which include open spaces of country,
sparsely populated, tend to underrate the effective density and reduce its
value as a relative measure of living conditions.
3. Distribution.
Under this heading the figures refer solelv to the civilian population
(Table VII).
The civilian population of the Maltese Islands was 24] ,621 distributed
as follows: —
Malta: Urban districts ... ... ... ... 55,439
Suburban districts ... ... ... ... 92,254
Rural districts ... ... ... ... 70,091
Gozo ... ... ... ... ... ... 23,837
Of the 43 urban, suburban and rural districts into which Malta is
divided, the population of the 5 urban districts amounted, at the date of
the Census, to 55,439 persons, while the population of the suburban
districts (numbering 13) was 92,254, and that of the rural districts
(numbering 25) was 70,091 persons. So that the proportion now living
in urban, suburban and rural conditions may broadly be regarded as 25.46,
42.36 and 32.18 per cent, respectively.
It is interesting to note that among the 43 districts in Malta there
are six each of which had in 1931 a population exceeding 10,000.
Altogether they account for 96,418 of the population of 241,621.
The following statement showing the population in urban, suburban
and rural districts and giving similar statistics for earlier Censuses, shows
the steadily increasing predominance of the suburban as compared with
the urban and rural population:
After 1901, when the proportions were about equal, the sub-urhan
element gained a definite lead, which was rapidly and consistently
increased in each decennium until it reached in 1931 nearly one-half of
the total population.
This movement of the population is due to the definite tendency of
migration from the urban and rural to sub-urban areas. It is clearly
traced in Table VIII, and it is mainly brought about by the rapid growth
of Sliema, St. Julian’s, Paula and Hamrun (with which are grouped S.
Vennera and Marsa). The population of these four sub-urban districts
in the last five censuses is shown in the following statement: —

Valletta and Floriana on the western side of the Grand Harbour
contained a population of 22,779 and 6,241 respectively; and the
population of Cottonera, on the eastern side, was 26,419, whereof 12,163
were enumerated at Cospicua, 7,683 in Senglea and 6,573 in Vittoriosa.
Thus out of a population of 217,784, 147,693 were living in the
fortified towns of Valletta, Floriana and Cottonera (55,439); and in the
suburbs(92,2s4). In other words 68%, or 17/25ths of the population of
Malta was massed in the neighbourhood of the towns and harbours, within
a radius of miles from Valletta.
A line skirting west of Gargur, Naxaro, Musta and Rabato and
thence south-east to Siggieui and Krendi would divide the island of
Malta into two halves. In the half east and north of that line 209,922
persons or 96.39 per cent, of the population were enumerated, whilst in
the other half 7,862 persons or 3.61 per cent, of the population were
enumerated. Practically, therefore, the whole of the population of Malta
is massed within a radius of six miles from the Grand Harbour, the hub
of work and commerce, and the focus of its economic life.
4. Births, Deaths and Marriages.
In dealing with Births, Deaths and Marriages the figures, as in the
case of “3 Distribution”, refer to the civilian population alone.
The births during the decennium totalled 75,976 and the deaths
51,162. There was, therefore, an excess of births over deaths of 24,814.
The figures for the Census taken in 1921 were: births 69,634; deaths
53,356; natural increment 16,278. The following table shows, by
sexes, the births and deaths in each year during the intercensal period
1921-1931 :
Grouping the two broken periods of 1921 and 1931 as one whole
year, the added births and deaths are: births 7,126, and deaths

It will be observed that thus the proportion of deaths to births
ranged from 72.70 in 1927 to 63.72 in 1926, the average yearly death-rate
per 100 live-births during the intercensal period being 67.35 as against
75.57 for the intercensal period 1911-1921.
The total deaths under one year of age registered in the Maltese
Islands during the intercensal period were 20,810, out ol 51,162 total
deaths, and the following statement shows these deaths in each year from
25th April, 1921, to 26th April, 1931 ;
Tim deaths under one year of age during the intercensal period were,
therefore, nearly 41 per cent, of the total deaths, and were equal to 27,39
per 100 registered live-births, whilst the death-rate among the whole
population was 67.35 per 100 live-births, so that the deaths over one year
were 39.94 per 100 live-births.
The number of marriages in the intercensal period was 14,354, an
increase of 1,140 on the number in the preceding period.
The yearly average of the number of persons married was 1,435.4 as
against 1,321.4 during the intercensal period 1911-1921.

Of these the figures relating to births and deaths, of which detailed
records are obtained under a careful system of registration, can be
accepted without reserve. The net migration shown is a net balancing
item to complete the table and may be taken as substantially correct.
In the following table the actual increase of the civilian population,
the actual increment and the calculated balance of passenger movement,
during the intercensal period 1921-1931, are shown, and comparative
figures for earlier Censuses are added.
Again the most noticeable feature of the foregoing table is the fall
in the" number of births, compensated, however, by the remarkable
decrease in the number of deaths. The balance of emigration and im-
migration is for the second time since 1871 in favour of immigration,
there being 4,549 more immigrants. The cause of the balance of movement
entailing not only a reduction of the normal outward flow but the reversal
of the net motion from an outward to an inward direction during the
intercensal period, may be ascribed mainly to the excess of inward over
outward migration of families of officers and other ranks or ratings of naval,
military and air force services, due in the main to the large increase in the
Mediterranean Fleet during the last decade. In a lesser degree it is
attributable to the fact that not only has emigration to the United States
of America and Australia ceased, but emigrants established in those countries
are returning to Malta in consequence of the trade depression.
The subjoined table shows the total increase in the civilian population
since 1828, when a regular Census was first taken.

The civilian population of the Maltese Islands, therefore, increased
by 125,676, or more than doubled itself in a century, the increase since
1828, viz : 103 years, being over 108 per cent. The rate of increase among
the more advanced countries which compile regular statistics works out at
over 1.1 per cent, yearly, a rate which would double the population in
about 60 years; but the drain of emigration has so far been so severe in
the Maltese Islands that notwithstanding a creditably high birth-rate, the
net population loss from this cause is responsible for the longer period
which was required. The high death-rate was also a factor militating in
this direction.
The variations in the urban, suburban and rural population of Malta,
and in the population of Gozo, during the last 50 vears, are shown in the
subjoined return :
The urban population which was 57,140 in 1881, was 55,439 in 1931,
a decrease of 1,701 or 2.98 per cent.; the suburban population which
numbered 30,532 in 1881, rose to 92,254 in 1931, an increase of 61,722 or
202.16 per cent.; and the rural population from 44,457 in 1881 increased
to 70,091 in 1931, a rise of 25,634 or 57.64 per cent.; the population of
Gozo from 1881 to 1931 increased by 6,184 or 35.03 per cent.; from 17,653
to 23,837.
It is noteworthy of record that in the intercensal period 1921-1931
an all-round increase was registered in urban, suburban and rural areas.
The rate of increase in the urban districts was 3.70 per cent., the highest

recorded since 1881; in the suburban districts 24.06 per cent., the lowest
excepting the period 1911-1921, which included the war years; and in the
rural districts 13.13 per cent., the highest excepting the period 1901-1911.
The population of Gozo increased by 5.07 per cent.
In Table VII are shown, in detail, by places, the numerical and
centesimal increases during the intercensal period 1921-1931. They range
from 62.61 per cent, in Marsa (Suburban) to 1.73 per cent, in Valletta
(Urban). Slight unimportant decreases are registered in Senglea (Urban)
and Safi (Rural) in Malta, and in Garbo, San Lorenzo and Nadur in Gozo.
Whilst the civilian population of Malta grew, during the last 103
years, by 117.07 per cent., the corresponding rate for the urban districts
was only 15.37 per cent., as compared with 554.85 per cent, for the suburban,
and 83.55 per cent, for the rural districts. The population of Gozo increased
during the same period by 52.62 per cent., the increase for both Islands
being 108.39 per cent.
The steady process of gravitation towards suburban areas has been
manifesting itself since 1861. It is in the main attributable to the overflow
of population from the fortified towns, and in a lesser degree to migration
from the rural districts and from Gozo, to localities nearer the harbours.
Sliema and Hamrun, which now form the principal suburbs of Valletta,
may be cited as outstanding examples of the rapid growth of the suburban
population. In 1861 the population of Sliema was 324. This microscopic
population has risen in 70 years to 18,880, an increase of 5,761 per cent.
The population of Hamrun (including S. Vennera and Marsa) during
the same period, has risen by 20,531, from 1,555 to 22,086, an increase
of 1.320 per cent.
In Table VIII the process of movement of population during the
intercensal period 1921-1931 is shown, by places, in each instance the
increase or decrease, as the case may be, by births over deaths and by
migration, being recorded. This Table graphically illustrates the large
scale movement from urban to suburban areas, and from Gozo to a lesser
extent.
In the following table are shown six districts (two urban and four
suburban) each with a population of over 10,000. The population in the
urban districts, viz: Valletta and Cospicua, has remained practically
stationary since 1891, whilst the population in the suburban districts shows
increases ranging from 261 to 37 per cent, during the period 1891-1931.
The population of Valletta which was 22,772 in 1828 and 22,779 in
1931. was at its peak in 1881 when it rose to 24,854 and at its lowest in
1921 when it numbered 22,392.
PART lII—INFANT MORTALITY.
At the 1911 Census, married persons were asked for the first time
to give particulars of children born alive during the intercensal period
and those of them who died, stating the age at death. This information
was required in order to obtain statistics of the number of deaths among
children born during the ten years preceding Census Day.

Tbe enquiry being a new one and somewhat complicated it was
realised that the resultant figures abstracted were far from accurate.
Recourse was therefore had to the records available in the Public Health
Department and the data published in the Census Report was obtained
therefrom with absolute precision. The same procedure was adopted in
the 19-1 Census; and in this Census this process was repeated, the
infant mortality” enquiry being omitted from the householders’ Schedule.
The number of children born during the intercensal period 1921-1931
totalled 75,976. Of these 24,181, or 31.83 per cent., died within the same
period. The proportions for Malta and Gozo are given below:
Malta—Born: 69,109—Died: 22,269 —Per cent.: 32.22.
Gozo —Born: 6,B67—Died: I,9l2—Per cent.: 27.84.
These deaths, shown in detail, by places, in Table IX, are analysed
into a series of age groups below:
Out of every 100 children born in the period from 25th April, 1921,
to 26th April, 1931, not more than 68 were alive on Census day. From
reference to Table IX it will be realised further that out of every 100
children born in the decade not more than 95 reached their first month;
not more than 83 reached their sixth month; not more than 74 reached
their first year; the survivors in the tenth year being 68 out of every 100.
The following were the proportions in the urban, suburban and rural
districts of Malta:
Urban Areas —Born: 16,737 —Died: 4,594 —Per cent.: 27.45.
Suburban Areas —Born: 28,952 —Died: 9,361 —Per cent.: 31.64.
Rural Areas —Born: 23,420—Died: B,3l4—Per cent.: 35.05.
The lowest ratio was therefore in the urban districts, and the
highest in the rural.
The subjoined statement shows the proportion of deaths in the centres
having a population of 1,000 and over :

Apart from Floriana, the statistical figures for which centre are
vitiated by the inclusion therein of deaths occurring in the Central Civil
Hospital, it will be observed that the rural districts, which as a group
give the highest average death-rate, also give the highest ratios for single
parishes and centres. Victoria with Kercem and S. Lucia, in Gozo, is
at the bead of the list with a death rate of 44.75 per cent., with Zeitun
(with which is included Marsascirocco) following closely, with a death-
rate of 43.66 per cent., the highest death-rate in Malta. Following
Zeitun come next in order Siggieui, with a death-rate of 43.30 per cent.,
Axiak and Birzebbugia—42.ss and Luca —40.36.
The lowest ratios were in the following places;—
The highest percentages of deaths among children during the inter-
censal period were mostly in the villages in the eastern part of the Island,
e.g. Luca, Zeitun, Axiak; and it strikes one with great force that this
should be so for the last three decades; whilst the central and western
villages record a much lower ratio of mortality.
Certain striking figures emerge from reference to Table IX, which
shows in detail the deaths among children born during the intercensal
period. While 4,034 died in the first month from birth, the average
monthly rate for the following five months is only 1,819, and the rate drops
to 1.100 between the sixth and twelfth months of age.
It will also be observed that there is a marked decrease in the deaths
among children after reaching the second year of life, and thereafter
deaths among children fall rapidly and consistently from 714 between the
second and third year to 284 between the third and fourth year and thus
gradually to only 9 deaths in the age between nine and ten years. It must
be borne in mind, however, that these statistics refer solely to the children
born during the decade and do not include the deaths, at the various ages
stated , of children born within the previous decennium. Statistics regarding
all infant mortality are given under Part ll—Population, Section 4—Births,
Deaths and Marriages.
PART IV—SEXES, AGES AND MARITAL CONDITIONS.
1. Sexes.
Of the 241,621 persons enumerated in the Maltese Islands in 1931,
117,457 were males and 124,164 were females, giving an excess of females
of 6.707, or a proportion of 1,057 females to each 1,000 males. In 1921
the females outnumbered the males by 6.768, or a proportion of 1,066 to
each 1,000 males.

Notwithstanding that more males are born than females, and the
proportion in the first three age-groups is in favour of the male sex, this
preponderance however diminishes rapidly and the relative proportions
of the sexes become transposed after about the age of 15 owing to the
heavier mortality among males.
After the earliest years migration also affects the sex balance, still
further reducing the male proportion. But the greatest disproportions of
all are those shown for the age-groups between 20 and 45, in which the
preponderance of females averages 1,136 per 1,000 males. This is due to
the enumeration of a large number of females, the wives of officers and
men o+’ the garrison and fleet. In the succeeding age-groups the
preponderance of females is somewhat reduced, but the rather lower rate
of mortality among females keeps the disproportion higher the older the
age-group.
The number of males and females in the three main age groups in
1901. 1911, 1921 and 1931 were as follows;
A feature of this return is the female excess in the age groups from
15 to 64 which decreasing slightly before 1911, rose from 1,521 in that
year to 8,061 ten years later, corresponding to an increase in the proportion
from 1.024 to 1,129 females per 1,000 males, and was 7.467 in 1931,
corresponding to 1,105 females for each 1,000 males.
The sex proportions for all ages, however, compare with that of other
European countries, where they range between 1,099 and 1,033 females for
each 1,000 males.
The variations in the sex constitution of the population are naturally
reflected in the rates of increase of each sex, and these variations for the

2. Ages.
It is doubtful whether the individual year tabulation, as a measure of
age in a given population, presents any marked advantage over the shorter


and more easily studied series of five-year age-groups, and, therefore, as in
the 1921 Census, a tabulation by the conventional quinquennial grouping
of age (0 —5, s—lo,5 —10, etc.,) has been provided for ages over 24, whilst under
that age the classification is given by individual years of age, and under the
age of one year the ages are tabulated in four three-monthly groups.
Table X shows the 1931 total civilian population in separate years
up to 24 and thereafter in quinquennial groupings, in respect of "each
town or village of Malta and Gozo, distinguishing males and females.
Table XI shows in three different groups, and in separate years
up to 24, and thereafter in quinquennial periods, the ages of the civilian
population of Malta and Gozo, distinguishing the sexes.
Table XTI shows the number of children, distinguishing males and
females, at each year of life up to 15 years, enumerated in Malta and
Gozo.
Fable XIII shows by districts (urban, suburban and rural) in three
different groups and also in quinquennial periods the ages of the civilian
population of Malta, males and females.
Table XIV shows, by districts, the number of children, males and
females, at each year of life up 15 years, enumerated in Malta.
Table XV shows the number of children, by sexes, at each year of
life up to 15 years, in respect of each town and village in Malta
and Gozo.
Table XVI shows the number of persons, males and females, in
several groups of ages, in 1931, and the numerical and centesimal increase
or decrease in each group during the last two decennary periods.
Table XVII shows the percentage of the civilian population, males
and females, in each group of ages in 1921 and 1931.
In considering the age constitution of the population of to-day or of
any other period it must be borne in mind that in itself it is nothing more
than a resultant distribution from the effect of factors pperating over
practically the wdiole of the preceding century. The population ranges
from 0 years beyond 100, so that the numbers in the several age-groups
in 193; are survivors of the births which have occurred since before the
year 1831, and it is apparent, therefore, that to the variations in the
numbers born in successive years and in their subsequent rates of survival
is mainly due the moulding of the present age distribution. It is not proposed
here to examine the birth and survival rates in any detail, but attention
may be drawn to the fact that a decline in the birth-rate has set in and
that the death-rate has also in recent years shown a decline.
In addition to the natural rorces of births and deaths there are large
and varying movements of population, usually referred to as emigration and
immigration, which occur chiefly during youthful and young adult age
periods. Also in our case Malta having a large military and naval
population the movements of the fugitive population, as distinct from the
permanent population, must be reckoned.
Of the total civilian population of 241,621, 77,495. or 321 per 1,000,
were under 15 years; 150,081, or 621 per 1,000, were of the ages of 15 to
64. and 14,045, or 58 per 1,000, were of the years of 65 upwards. The
figures for 1921 are so nearly equal as to render any comment
thereon obviously unnecessary, but it is safe to attribute the slight increase
in the youngest age-group to the lightening of the mortality affecting
them.

there were in each of the three main age-groups in 1851 and at each
successive Census:—

T 1 e proportion of persons per 1,000 of population since 1851 has hardly
•changed. The proportion in the age-groups under 15 was 324 in 1851,
rose to 341 in 1901, fell to 318 in 1921 and came up to 321 in 1931, a
decrease since 1851 of 3 per 1,000; the proportion in the intermediate age-
groups from 614 in 1851 rose to 645 in 1861, fell to 603 in 1891, again rose
to 626 in 1921 and again fell to 621 in 1931, there being an increase of 7
per 1,000 since 1851; and the proportion in the age-groups from 65 and
upwards show somewhat wide oscillations from 62 in 1851 to 59 in 1871,
to 71. in 1881, down to 53 in 1901, with a continuous rise to 58 in 1931,
there being ultimately a decrease of 4 per 1,000 since 1851.
While in 1921 the population under 15 years of age constituted 31.76
per cent, of the total, the proportion in 1931 rose to 32.07. In the
intermediate age-groups (15 to 64) there was a decline of 0.48 per cent.,
with 62.12 in 1931 against 62.60 in 1921, and in the age-groups of 65 and
upwards was recorded a slight increase of 0.17, the figures being 5.81 and
5.64 for 1931 and 1921 respectively.
The population under 15 increased by 10,075 or 14.94 per cent, on the
number of the same age returned in 1921; the increase was 17,214, or
12.96 in the intermediate age-groups, as against 2.39 per cent, in 1921;
and in the higher age-groups an increase of 2,074 was recorded or 17.33
per cent, against 12.24 per cent, in 1921.
Population is divided into two main age-groups, viz: the effective age-
groups. 15 to 64, and the dependent ages, o—ls and 65 and over, and the
following table shows these two age-groups as they were in 1931 and in
1911 and 1921.
It will be observed that a slight decrease in the percentage of population
in the effective ages is shown in 1931, made up by a corresponding increase
in the percentages in the dependant ages. This may be ascribed to a lower
death-rate and an average rising duration of life, which are more important
than a high birth-rate.

population enumerated since 1851 shows how the processes of increase
and decrease act on each other in the course of years.

Comparing the same group of ages through the series of decades, it will
be seen, for example, that the number of children under 10 years of age
increased from 28,392 in 1851 to 49,231 in 1911, reacting down to 43,702
in 1921 and again taking the upward curve to 54,701 in the last decennium.
This figure is made up of the survivors of the children born in the
intercensal period, viz : 75,976 births less deaths of children under 10 years
of age; plus children under 10 years of age belonging to the fugitive
population. The table also shows, for instance,,that other notable increases
in the last intercensal period were among adults in the age-groups 20 to 29.
30 to 39 and 40 to 49; and in the dependant age-groups 70 to 89.
By reading the table horizontally it is possible to follow the gradual
thinning of the population, as each group passes from one age-group to
the other. For example, of the 28,392 who were under 10 years of age in
1851 there were in 1931 only 1,716 survivors, now aged from 80 to 89
years. This means, in other words, that out of every 1,000 persons only
60 reach the age of between 80 and 89. The table below illustrates graphically
the chances of life between the ages of 40 and 89.
The subjoined return shows the sex constitution of the population in

One point emerges forcibly from a contemplation of the above figures,
namely, that in the intercensal period, there was an increase in the number
of males in all the stages of life; and an increase in the number of females,
except for a slight decrease in the ages between 15 and 44.
3. Marital Conditions.
Of the 241,621 persons enumerated in 1931, 149,233 were returned as
single, 79,649 as married and 12,739 as widowed. Among the single of all
ages there were 979 females to 1,000 males; the number of wives enumerated
exceeded the number of husbands by 3,369 and were in proportion of 1,088
wives to 1,000 husbands; the number of widows was 8,835, as compared
with 3,904 widowers. In 1921 the ratio of single females to single males of
all ages was 988 to 1,000; the wives exceeded the husbands by 3,094 and
were in the proportion of 1,092 to 1,000; and the widows numbered 8,026
against 3,594 widowers.
In Tables XVIII, XIX, XX, XXI and XXII the numbers returned
under each condition in 1931 are set out in age groups, and in the following
table the numbers returned under each condition in 1921 and in 1931 are
set out and the intercensal increases or decreases show where the changes
have occurred.
The excess of unmarried males, which was 758 in 1921, increased to
1,593 in 1931; the number of wives exceeded that of husbands by 3,369
against 3,094 in 1921; and the widows exceeded the widowers by 4,931
against 4,432 in 1921.
The percentage of the unmarried of both sexes increased by 0.33
and 0.18 for males and females respectively; there was a slight increase
in the proportion of married females, viz: 0.03 per cent.; and a decrease in
the proportion of married and of widowed males and widowed females, viz;
0.12 and 0.18, and 0.21 per cent, respectively. The variations, however,
are not such as to warrant any comment thereon, but may be definitely
stated to be due to the change in the sex constitution of the total population.
The position of the sex constitution of the population would be better
visualised if the proportions were based on the adult population only.
Leaving out children under 15, who numbered 39,334 males and 38,161
females, from the total of 117,457 males and 124,164 females, the resultant-
number of males and females of 15 years of age and over would be 78,123
and 86.003 respectively. Of these, 36,079 males and 35,660 females ware
single; 38,140 males and 41,508 females were married; and 3,904 males
and 8,835 females were widowed. On this basis the rate per hundred would
be as follows, the correspending rate for 1921 being added.

Here again although the figures differ the variations during the
intercensal period follow closely the same incidence in the three categories.
The following return shows the variations between 1911 and 1931 in
the population in each class, to the adult population of each sex;—
Number and Proportion per 100 of each Sex, 15 years and upwards
In the ages of 15 and upwards, there were per 100 males of each class:
in 1911, 91 spinsters, 102 wives and 229 widows; in 1921, 102 spinsters,
109 wives and 223 widows; and in 1931, 99 spinsters, 109 wives and 226
widows.
During the intercensal period, therefore, compared with the preceding
period, there was a decrease in the proportion of spinsters and widows per
100 bachelors and widowers, while no change occurred in the proportion
of wives per 100 husbands.
In view of the tendency to early marriages hitherto obtaining in the
Maltese Islands it will be of interest to compare the results of the present
Census with those of previous Censuses as to the proportion of married and
widowed between the ages of 15 and 24.
Percentage of married and widowed, 15 to 24 years of age, to persons
of same sex and age, 1891 to 1931.
It Avi]] be seen that the proportion of husbands and widowers in the
early ages has been steadily declining during the last four decades, and has
diminished from 12.5 in 1891 to 7.3 in 1931, a decrease of 5.2, or 41.6 per
cent. The proportion of wives and widows also declined from 24.2 in 1891
to 17.3 in 1931, a decrease of 6.9. or 28.1 per cent.
From this marked steady fall for both sexes, even after making
allowance in respect of male-emigration, which is comparatively large in
this period of life, clearly emerges the point that early marriages have been
declining gradually since 1891.
Out of every 100 males between the ages of 15 and 44, 41.0 per cent,
were married or widowed, against 41.1 in 1921, and 48.4 per cent, of the
females in the same age-group were married or widowed, in both the 1921
and 1931 Censuses. In the ordinary marriageable ages, therefore, there
also was practically no change in the proportions for both sexes.

PART V—BUILDINGS, DWELLINGS, ROOMS AND FAMILIES.
The detailed examination of the buildings, which is necessary at a
Census to ensure the complete enumeration of all population, provides an
opportunity for the collection of valuable data in regard to the housing
accommodation of the people and of the type of buildings in which they
are distributed. v J
Each Enumerator employed at the Census was instructed to explore
carefully the whole of his district; to enter in his enumeration register the
type of each building as he visited it on his route; to ascertain whether it
was actually used or intended for habitation; and to say how many rooms
the dwelling contained and note the number of persons living in each
inhabited dwelling.
The total number of buildings enumerated (Tables XXIII, XXIV and
was 79,852, whereof 51,400 were inhabited and 28,452 uninhabited
Ihe figures in 1921 were 68,485, 44,991 and 23,494 respectively. During
the mtercensal period, therefore, the number of inhabited buildings
increased by 6,409, equivalent to 14.25 per cent., while the uninhabited
buildings increased by 4,958, or 21.10 per cent. The numerical and
centesimal increase in the mtercensal period 1911-1921 was 2,193, or 5.12
per cent., for inhabited buildings, and 4,785, or 25.58 per cent, for
uninhabited buildings. The total number of buildings exceeded that of
1921 by 11,367, or 16.6 per cent. The increase in 1921 was 6.978 or 11 35
per cent.
The following table shows the increase or decrease, during the
inteicensal period, of all buildings, shown under their various descriptions,
and the variation in comparison with the figures in 1921:
The number of persons enumerated in dwellings in 1931 was 233,530
and 12,089 in Institutions. The figures in 1921 were 206,364 and
respectively. The increase recorded was, therefore, 27,166 in dwellings and
1,851 in Institutions.

In Malta there were 70,611 buildings, of which 45,792 were inhabited
and 24,819 uninhabited. These figures show an increase over those of the
previous Census as follows :
All buildings ... ... 10,862 or 18.18 per cent.
Inhabited buildings ... 6,004 or 15.09 ~. ~
Uninhabited buildings ... 4,858 or 24.39 ~ ~
The total number of buildings existing in Gozo was 9,241, made up of
5,608 inhabited, and 3,633 uninhabited. The increases over the results of
the previous Census are as is shown hereunder :
All buildings ... ... ... 505 or 5.78 per cent.
Inhabited buildings ... ... 405 or 7.78 ~ ~
Uninhabited buildings ... ... 100 or 2.83 ~ ~
The remarkable increase in the number of buildings, both inhabited and
uninhabited, during the intercensal period, is a welcome sign of the revival
of the building trade. The standard of living has risen considerably
accompanied by a higher standard of comfort. Thus an increased demand
has been created, among other things, for more roomy houses, and, apart
from new buildings erected in the growing suburbs, buildings were also
erected in practically all parts of the Island. At the time of the Census
over 500 buildings were in course of erection, each town or village, however
small, in Malta or Gozo, with the exception of Notabile, having its quota
of new buildings in course of construction.
In the urban districts inhabited buildings increased by 669 and the
uninhabited buildings by 583, while the increase in the suburban districts
was 3,614 for inhabited buildings and 2,484 for uninhabited buildings. In
the rural districts the inhabited buildings increased by 1,721 and the
uninhabited by 1,791.
Uninhabited houses, as distinct from buildings not used as dwellings,
numbered 8,606, made up of 618 in the urban districts, 3,092 in the suburban
districts, 3,573 in the rural districts and 1,323 in Gozo. The figures for
1921 were :
Urban ... ... ... ... 542
Suburban ... ... ... ... 2,494
Rural ... ... ... ... 2,840
Gozo ... ... ... ... 1,308
7,184
These buildings were mainly unoccupied residences in the summer resorts
of Birzebbugia, Marsascala, Marsascirocco, St. Julian’s, Sliema and St.
Paul’s Bay in Malta, which account for 3,419, and Marsalforno in Gozo,
with 347 unoccupied houses; and houses to be let furnished in Sliema.

The proportion of the population living in Institutions, and the laro-e

private dwellings separately from Institutions
SS nber of persons enumerated in buildings other than Institutions
was 233.530 in 51,220 dwellings. This gives a density of habitation of
4.56 persons to a dwelling, against 4.60 in 1921 and 4.84 in 1914. Judged
by this test therefore, there was a sensible improvement in the standard
oi comfort during the last two decades.

For Census purposes a family is considered to be all the persons
enumerated on a given Schedule, and includes boarders, servants sleeping-in,
guests, etc. The number of private families is therefore equal to the
number or Schedules collected, other than those referring to Institutions.
The total number of families living in private buildings was 54 006
against 48,116 m 1921 and 45,594 in 1911. The increase during the
mtercensal period 1921-1931 was, therefore, 5,890 families, against an
increase of 2.522 in the decennium 1911-1921.
The average number of families to an inhabited building, was 1.05 as
f gamS M 07 111 was 106 in Malta, as against 1.08 in 1921, and
I.no m Gozo which is the same as in 1921.
In the urban districts the average was 1.21, in the suburban 1 02 and
m the rural 1.01. The figures for 1921 were 1.26, 1.03 and 102
respectively.

N.B.—The figures in italics indicate the population living in overcrowded dwellings.
These figures reveal a decided movement towards better housing
conditions among the population. The ratio of families living in one room
dropped from 11.18 in 1921 to 8.46 per cent, of the total families living in
buildings other than Institutions. The ratio of families living in two-roomed
houses dropped from 28.67 to 23.51. The ratio for three-roomed houses
increased from 19.00 per cent, to 21.96; for four-roomed houses from 14.85
per cent, to 19.00 and that for five-roomed houses and over from 26.28 per
cent, to 27.07.
The above table indicates a notable rise all through in the standard
of comfort in housing. 68.03 per cent, of the total number of families were
living on Census day in three- or more-roomed houses as against 60.15 in
the 1921 Census, and only 31.97 per cent, of the total number of families
were living in one- or two-roomed houses as against 39.85 in 1921.
As each family is considered to be living in a separate tenement, the
number of tenements may, for practical purposes, be taken as being equal
to that of the number of families. Hence, the proportion of inhabited
buildings of each size is that given for families in the table above. It may,
therefore, be taken that 8.46 per cent, of the inhabited buildings were
one-roomed, 23.51 were two-roomed, and so on.
The number of families living in an overcrowded condition, based on
the standard of more than two persons per room, in a tenement of less than
five rooms, was 8,925, consisting of 59,429 persons, which is equal to an
average of 6.66 persons to a family, and to 16.53 of the total number of
families enumerated.
In 1921 the corresponding figures were as follows :
Number of families in overcrowded dwellings ... 9,188
Number of persons ... ... ... ... ... 58,548
Average number of persons to a family ... ... 6.37
Percentage of families in overcrowded dwellings on
total number of families ... ... ... ... 19.07
There was, therefore, a sensible decrease in the number of families
living in overcrowded dwellings, but a slight increase in the number of
persons represented by the same. On the whole, however, there is room for
improvement, as the number of families occupying overcrowded dwellings,
suggest a considerable population living in inadequate dwellings.
Table XXVI shows the distribution of families in dwellings of different
sizes. Mutiplying the number of persons in the family by the number of
families, as shown in that table, we arrive at the population living in each
class of tenement. The result is shown in the following statement:—

Marsascirocco (48.9 per cent. )and Valletta (40.0 per cent.) in Malta; and

The highest increases were recorded in Zeitun (10.0 per cent.) and Luca

there were decreases in nearly all important centres, the figures for districts
in Malta being 34.8, 23.2 and 22.5 in 1921 as against 30°4, 20.4 and 19.7
in 1931 In Gozo the figure of 28.0 per cent, in 1921 fell to 24.2 per cent,
in 1931. The figure for both Islands from 26.3 per cent, in 1921, fell to 22.8
per cent, in 1931.
Taking the average number of persons in overcrowded dwellings to a
room, it will be observed that the highest increase was in Musta, where the
average of 2.3 persons to a room in 1921 rose to 3.0. The average for
Valletta was the same as in 1921. In other centres the variations were not
of much significance, but they mostly indicate an improvement in housing

Nearly 26 per cent, of the population of Valletta lived in “Kerrejas”
(tenement buildings), Floriana coming next with 17.23 per cent. The
following statement shows the proportion of the population living in
“Kerrejas” and the average number of occupants per room.
The total population enumerated in “Kerrejas” was 11,821, against
10,561, in 1921; or 5.06 per cent, of the population in private families,
against 5.13 in 1921. In 1911 the figures were 12,653 and 6.13 per cent.
Thus in two decades the population living in “Kerrejas ” fell hy 832.
Nevertheless the proportion of the population living in “Kerrejas” is
still high, although it is of interest to note that there is practically no
abnormal overcrowding in these tenements. The highest ratio in 1931 was,
in fact, 3.19 occupants per inhabited room, in Marsa, where there were
four “Kerrejas” with 249 persons. The average for 5,942 persons in the
Valletta “Kerrejas” was only 2.27 per inhabited room. It was 2.45 in
Floriana, 2.09 in Misida, 2.07 in Sliema and Gzira and 2.02 in Melleha.
In all the other places the average was under two occupants per inhabited
room.

in previous Censuses, the occupations for classification purposes were
divided into six orders, as follows:
I. Professional
11. Personal
111. Commercial
IV. Agricultural
V. Industrial
VI. Unoccupied and unproductive.
n Malta, as in all other countries of the same size, many persons are
engaged m more than one vocation, and as the same individual in the
lables of Occupations cannot be entered under more thon one head, persons
pursuing more than one occupation were returned under that by which
they mainly earned their livelihood. For this reason the results of the
abstraction of ‘Occupations’ can only be regarded as the nearest possible
approach of the real figures.
The Returns of Occupations (Tables XXVIII and XXIX) deal with
persons of the age of 5 years and upwards. The population of 5 years and
over, whmh was 189,288 in 1921, was in 1931, 213,503, an increase of
24,215, or 12.79 per cent, during the decade.
The following is a comparative statement of the persons enumerated
under each of the orders in 1921 and in 1931. with the relative variation:
Of a total population of the age of 5 years and over, 103 239 were
males and 110,264 were females. Of the males 68,346. or 66.20 per cent
followed a vocation and 34,893, or 33.80 per cent., were unoccupied. Of
tlie females 18,502, or 16.78 per cent, were occupied and 91,762 or 83 22
per cent., were unoccupied. The figures for 1921 were:
In the male element, therefore, there was practically no change in the
proportion of occupied and unoccupied sections of the population: but
m the .emale element a decrease of 5.24 was registered in the occupied
section, which is shown naturally as an increase in the unoccupied section
This may be ascribed to the larger number of the female fugitive population
(wives of Officers and other ranks or ratings of the Garrison and Fleet!
iving oil the Island on Census day and who are ail shown under the
unoccuDied section.


It may consequently be deduced that productiveness has risen in
proportion to the increase in the population during the intercensal period
1921-1931.
Order I. Professional.
The professional class which was made up of 8,032 males and 1,607
females in 1921, a total of 9,639 persons, was 10,269 in 1931, viz; 8,069
males and 2,200 females. The total number of persons in this Order was
4.8 per cent of the total population of 5 years and over, against 5.1 per
cent, in 1921, a decrease of 0.3 per cent.
This order is divided into three sub-orders, viz:
1. Local Government with 1,545 males and 15 females
2. Army and Navy with 2,283 males
3. Professional with 4,241 males and 2,185 females.
Sub-order 1 is composed of the higher and lower administrative
employees and Police, but does not include those employees exercising a
trade or profession, who are classed under their specific designations. In
this sub-order there was an increase of 352 males and a decrease of 13
females.
Sub-order 2 includes local troops, civilians, not otherwise returned,
employed with the Military, Maltese in the Royal Navy, and Dockyard
Officials and other employees, exclusive of skilled workmen who are included
under more specific sub-orders. In this sub-order the males decreased by
1,003, the decrease being mainly due to fewer local troops and partly to
the employment of a lesser number of men in the Dockyard.
In sub-order 3 there was an increase of 688 males and 611 females,
these increases being mainly in the legal, medical and teaching professions
and in the engineering professions and allied trades.
There were 643 Roman Catholic Secular Clergy against 718 in 1921,
a decrease of 75; the Monastic Orders totalled 506, against 446 in 1921, an
increase of 60; and the number of professed Nuns and Sisters was 940,
against 747 in 1921, an increase of 193.
Order 11. Personal or Domestic.
This order in mainly* made up of domestic servants and includes also
those engaged in personal services, such as hairdressers, barbers, etc. It
consisted of 3,154 males and 6,029 females, the domestic services alone
accounting for 1,550 males and 4,741 females. The figures in 1921 were
2,657 males and 4,965 females, thus there was an increase of 497 males
and 1.064 females. In 1921 domestic services accounted for 1,340 males and
3,861 females, the increase being therefore 210 males and 880 females.
Order 111. Commercial.
22,533 persons, viz: 19,975 males and 2,558 females were engaged in
commercial pursuits, showing an increase of 2,085 males and 108 females
over the corresponding figures for 1921.
Thi> order is divided into three sub-orders as follows:
1. Merchants, dealers and agents ... 11,163 persons
2. Persons connected with commerce ... 3,506 persons
3. Conveyance ... ... ... ... 7,864 persons
In 1921 the total number of persons employed in each of these sub-
divisions was respectively 10,084, 2,311 and 7,945. There was an increase,
therefore, of 1,079 in sub-order 1, of 1,195 in sub-order 2, and a decrease of
81 in sub-order 3.

Order IV. Agricultural.
In agriculture during the decade 1921-1931 the trend has been steadily
in a downard direction; agricultural workers having decreased in number
by 2 607, from 16,232 in 1921 to 13,625 in 1931, the decrease being made
up of 1,170 males and 1,437 females. This decrease is shown in detail in
the following statement: —
This all-round decrease totalling 2,607, or 16 per cent., is mainly due
to the unremunerative wages paid for agricultural labour and the consequent
result of such labour being diverted to more remunerative lines of work,
such as stone-dressers, etc., when there is a demand for such labour. Owing
to the great number of buildings which are being erected in all parts of the
Island, this demand has increased to such an extent that it is drawing on
agricultural labour. In the case of the female element it can be stated with
•certainty that this class of labour is being drawn upon for domestic service.
Order V. Industrial.
This, the most important among the orders of occupation, included
31,184 persons, viz: 25,929 males and 5,255 females. The corresponding
figures in 1921 were 29,068, viz: 20,320 males and 8,748 females. Thus
there was an increase of 5,609 males, or 27.6 per cent., and a decrease of
3,493 females, or 39.9 per cent.
A decrease in the number of females engaged in occupation falling
under this order has been going on since 1911 when the decrease recorded
was 3,089, or 27.4 per cent. In 1921 there was a slight increase of 570,
or 6.97 per cent., but again in 1931 the decrease reached the total of 3,493,
or 39 9 per cent.
Order V is divided into nine sub-orders the most important of which
are those of “Metal Workers”, “Stone and Lime workers”, “Workers in
Wood and Furniture”, “Textile and Dress”, “Food, Drink and Tobacco”,
and “Other Workers undefined”, and in each of these sub-orders an
increase is shown with the notable exceptions of an unimportant decrease
of 118 under “Metal Workers” and a most striking decrease of 3,162 under
“Textile and Dress”, made up mostly of “Laceworkers”.

females) in the number of lace workers.
The practical extinction of the cotton spinning industry which formerly
flourished in these Islands, is strikingly illustrated in the following figures:
Cotton spinners and cotton weavers who numbered 14,446 in 1851
were reduced to the microscopical figure of 181 in 1931.
A disquieting feature in the 1931 Census is the decrease of 3.970 in the
lace-working industry. In 1911 lace workers numbered 4,801, which figure
was increased to 5,902 in 1921. The number is now 1,932. This notable
decrease is ascribed to the decreased demand for Malta lace due to the
manufacture of imitation Malta lace in European countries.
Included in one or other of the above Orders of Occupations were 15,035
persons who were returned as employed in Naval, Military and Civil
Government establishments. Of these 8,125 were employed in the
Dockyard and other Naval Establishments, 1,423 in Military Establish-
ments and 5,487 were shown on the Census Schedule as working under the
local Maltese Government.
The total number of persons working on their own account was 33,851
of whom 3,212 were employers of labour and accounted for the employment
of 32,355 persons.

It will be seen firom the above table that persons returned as living on
their own means decreased by 1,013; Pensioners increased by 266;
the number of scholars increased by 8,132; the number of children who had
no occupation and did not attend school decreased by 4,479; inmates in
Institutions increased by 485; the number of persons retired from profession,
business or trade increased by 1,262; the number of persons having no stated
occupation, which includes children of 13 and over not attending school,
increased by 16,201 of which 14,835 were females; and mendicants decreased
in number by 438, from 638 to 200.
It is relevant to remark here that the total number of persons in the
unoccupied and unproductive order included persons living on their own
means and pensioners; but although these persons are unoccupied, they are
not a burden on the rest of the population and should not, therefore, be
classed as forming part of the dependent section of the population.
Again, many females who are shown as unproductive because they do
not follow a vocation are wives and grown up daughters who are engaged in
the domestic duties of their household and in point of fact contribute their
fair share of useful work.
2. Unemployment.
The number of persons returned as unemployed on Census day
(26th April, 1931), was 3,048, whereof 2,878 were males and 170 were
females. This was 1,885 fewer than the previous Census, the figures for
1921 being 4,655 males and 278 females, or a total of 4,933.
These 3,048 persons unemployed were of all grades and classes and
may be analysed as follows ;
The proportion of unemployed to the total number of persons returned
under the above orders of occupations was 3.51 per cent, as against 5.94 per
cent, in 1921. For males alone the figures were 4.21 in 1931 and 7.60 in
1921. For the several orders the proportions were as follows;
As in 1921, industrial occupations, therefore, showed the highest
proportion as well as the greatest number of unemployed, while the profes-
sional and agricultural classes, also as in 1921. appeared to he the least
affected by unemployment.
The incidence of unemployment as might be expected was highest
amongst unskilled labourers, of whom there were 778 workless. In
there were 1,136 unemployed unskilled labourers, but the building industry
absorbs a number of unskilled labour and to-day a great many unskilled
labourers are being employed in the building trade. (*)
(*) The Census was taken on the 26th April, 1931, when the general economic depression had
not been felt in Malta.


PART VII —EDUCATION.
In the 1931 Census the method followed in the Census of 1921 was
adopted; the same questions were asked and the information obtained is
tabulated in identical tables to those printed in 1921, in a simplified form.
The figures given for persons having a knowledge of “any other language”
in Table XXX refer to those persons who, not speaking English or Italian,
possess a knowledge of another language and could not, therefore, be
classified under the heading “No Knowledge”.
Of a total Maltese population of 207,477 persons, five years of age and
upwards, 59,588, or 28.73 per cent., were returned as being able to speak
English, Italian or any other foreign language. The figure for 1921, with
a Maltese population, of five years and over, of 185,873, was 42,577, or
22.91 per cent., so that there was an increase of 5.82 per cent, in the number
of persons able to speak any language other than Maltese.
No doubt, some of these persons who returned themselves as speaking
either English or Italian, possessed probably only a superficial knowledge
of the language they professed to speak, but the Tables drawn up give
statistical figures abstracted from the schedules filled in by, or for, the
persons to whom they refer and these must be accepted at their face value.
English was spoken by 54,700 persons or 26.36 per cent, and Italian
by 32,416 persons or 15.62 per cent. The figures in 1921 were English
36,774 or 19.78 per cent.; Italian 25,329 or 13.63 per cent.; and there was,
therefore, an increase of 17,926 in the number of persons who spoke English;
and an increase of 7,087 in the number of persons who spoke Italian.
In 1921 the number of persons speaking English only was 17,106, or
9.20 per cent., the number of those speaking Italian only was 5,661, or 3.05
per cent., and that of those speaking both English and Italian was 19,668
or 10.58 per cent. In 1931 the figures were: 26,978, or 13.0 per cent.,
4,694, or 2.26 per cent., and 27,722, or 13.36 per cent. These figures show
an increase of 9,872 in the number of persons speaking English only and
an increase of 8,054 in the number of persons speaking both English and
Italian. A decrease of 967 in the number of persons speaking Italian only
is shown.
Table XXX shows, in detail, by places, and in age groups, the number
of persons able to speak English, Italian, both English and Italian, and
any other language, and -also the number of those persons who could not
speak any language besides Maltese. Tables XXXII and XXXIII show
the figures given in Table XXX, percentually and numerically, m age-
groups and by districts respectively.
Of a total Maltese population of 207,477, five years of age and upwards,
38,613 were able to write Maltese, 47,575 were able to write another
language, and 126,289 were unable to write in any language. The figures
in 1921 were 18,135, 36.666 and 131,072 respectively. Therefore there was
an increase of 15,478 in the number of persons able to write Maltese, an
increase of 10,909 in the number of persons able to write any other language,
and a decrease of 4,783 in the number of persons unable to write in any
language. Table XXXI shows in detail, by places and in groups of ages,
the number of persons able to write Maltese, or any other language, and the
number of persons unable to write in any language; and in Tables XXXIV
and XXXV the figures are set out percentually and numerically, in age-
groups and by districts.
The outstanding feature during the decade has been the increase in
the number of persons who were able to write Maltese which rising from
10,175 in 1911 to 18,135 in 1921, was 33,613 in 1931.
School age is assumed to be between 4 and 14 years, both inclusive.
The number of children of school age was returned at 55,007 of whom
27,982 were boys and 27,025 were girls (Table XXXVI). Of these 32,585, or

wschool going children over the corresponding figure of 1921.
Of the total of 27.982 boys, 17,417, or 62.24 per cent., attended school,
as compared with 53.41 per cent, in 1921, a rise of 8.83 per cent. Of the
27.025 girls, 15,168, or 56.13 per cent., attended school, against 49.90 per
cent, in 1921, a rise of 6.23 per cent.
Of the 49,778 children of school age in Malta, 28,995, or 58,25 per
cent., were receiving instruction, against 51.47 per cent, in 1921. In Gozo
of 5,229 children of school age, 3,590, or 68.66 per cent., were receiving
instruction, against 53.31 per cent, in 1921.
15,540, or 61.36 per cent, of the male children of school age, and

Malta. The corresponding figures for Gozo were 1,877, or 70.62 per cent.,
for bovs. and 1,713, or 66.63 per cent., for girls. The corresponding
percentages for 1921 were 53.30 and 49.57 for Malta, and 54.30 and 52.30
for Gozo.
The proportion of children of school age receiving instruction at Gozo
exceeded the corresponding figures for Malta by 9.26 per cent, in the case of
boys and by 11.61 per cent, in the case of girls and for both boys and girls
by 10.37 per cent.

43.44 per cent, of the children in the age-groups 4 to 9, and 36.97 per

These proportions are still very high although they show a substantial
improvement over the previous Census in the higher age-group.
The decrease in the proportion of children of the ages 4-9 attending
school is greatly to be deprecated and it is difficult to hazard an opinion
thereon.
PART VIII —BIRTHPLACE AND NATIONALITY.
Of the 241,621 persons constituting the civilian population of Malta
and Gozo, 233,035, or 96.45 per cent., were born in these Islands and 8,586,
or 3.55 per cent., were born abroad. (Tables XXXVIII —XLVI).
Of the native born population 62,757, or 25.97 per cent., were born in
the urban districts, 71,140, or 29.48 per cent., were born in the suburban
districts, and 72,791, or 30.13 per cent., in the rural districts. Those born
in Gozo numbered 26,347, or 10.90 per cent.
The urban districts retained only 44,703, or 71.23 per cent., of the
persons born therein. 15,409, or 24.55 per cent., moved to suburban
districts, 2,418, or 3.85 per cent., migrated to the rural districts and
227. or 0.36 per cent., crossed over to Gozo.
Of 71,140 persons born in the suburban districts, 63,798, or 89.68 per
cent., were residing in the same districts on Census Day; 4,537, or 6.38 per
cent., migrated to the urban districts; 2,586, or 3.63 per cent., moved to the
rural districts, and 219, or 0.31 per cent., crossed over to Gozo.
Of 72,791 persons born in the rural districts, 63,843, or 87.71 per cent.,
were still residing therein on Census Day; 2,479, or 3.40 per cent., migrated
to the urban districts; 6,288, or 8.64 per cent., moved to the suburban
districts; and 181, or 0.25 per cent., crossed over to Gozo.
Gozo retained 23,124, or 87.77 per cent., of its local born population
of 26,347. The remaining 3,223 migrated to Malta as follows:
870, or 3.30 per cent., to urban districts
1,926, or 7.31 per cent., to suburban districts
427, or 1.62 per cent., to rural districts.
The 8,586 persons born abroad were residing as follows:
2,850, or 33.19 per cent., in urban districts
4,833, or 56.29 per cent., in suburban districts
817, or 9.52 per cent., in rural districts
86, or 1.00 per cent., in Gozo.
So that on Census Day the civilian population was distributed as
follows:
55,439 in urban districts
92 254 in suburban districts
70,091 in rural districts
23,837 in Gozo.

As in 1921 Gozo and the suburban districts retained the highest
proportion of the population born therein, viz: 87.77 per cent., in the case
of Gozo, and 89.68 per cent, in the case of the suburban districts. In 1921
the figure was 89 per cent, in both. The rural districts came next with
87.71 per cent, as against 86.6 per cent, in 1921, and the urban districts
last with 71.23 per cent., as against 72.6 per cent, in 1921.
The number of emigrants from, and immigrants in, the various
districts is given in the following return:
The trend of migration to the suburban districts was most pronounced.
They were in fact the only districts in which the immigrants exceeded the
emigrants. All the other districts contributed by an excess of emigrants over
immigrants to the growth of the population of the suburban districts.
The total number of persons born abroad forming part of the civilian
population was 8,586, whereof 3,343 were males and 5,243 females. 7,638
(2,955 males and 4,683 females) were British Subjects, and 948 (388 males
and 560 females) were foreigners.
Of the British Subjects 2,124, viz: 1,028 males and 1,096 females, were
of Maltese nationality, while 5,514 (1,927 males and 3,587 females) were
non-Maltese.
Of the 948 foreigners 567 were born in Italy and 31 in Lybia.
1 he number of foreigners making Malta their home has been decreasing
since 1901 It was 2,363 in that year, 1,857 in 1911 and 1,298 in 1921.
The number of persons born in Italy and Lybia residing in Malta is also
decreasing. It was 1,924 in 1901, 1,387 in 1911, and 887 in 1921.
Foreigners formed 0.39 per cent, of the total civilian population. In
1921 they formed 0.61 per cent, of the total. There was, therefore,
a decrease of 0.22 per cent.
Of the total 948 foreigners 293 males and 301 females were engaged in
useful occupations (Table LI), and 69 males and 234 females were not

PART IX—WORKPLACES.
At the 1931 Census occupied persons were asked for the first time to
give particulars of their place of work. The enquiry being a new one and
so many of the ■working population in Malta have no fixed place of work
that the treatment and arrangement of the data collected must be regarded
in a measure as experimental.
The form of the statement hereunder has largely been governed to show
the day and night population of the urban and suburban areas, persons

Marsa

and al) exits from each area to the ‘‘working” centres. The movements
shown therein obviously have a direct bearing upon many difficult problems
of traffic. It must be remembered, however, that the ‘‘exits” refer to
occupied persons only and do not include scholars attending school outside
the areas in which they reside.
PART X—MISCELLANEOUS.
1. Maltese abroad.
In Table LIV an abstract is given of the returns which it has been
possible to obtain of the Maltese British Subjects resident in certain
countries bordering on the Mediterranean and in certain other countries.
These returns, however, cannot be regarded in any way as complete.
A considerable number of Maltese fail to register in the Consulates
Some of the figures are merely an estimate.
Although it was not practicable to obtain returns from certain countries
in which it was known that a considerable number of Maltese reside (as. for


example, Detroit), it has been possible to ascertain that 35,089 Maltese,
who have retained their British Nationality, were living in the countries
shown in Table LIV on or about Census Day. Of these 19,736 were males
and 15,353 females. Bearing in mind that the returns are incomplete, this
figure may fairly be taken as the minimum estimate of the Maltese British
Subjects residing abroad.
2. Civilian Institutions.
The number of persons enumerated in certain types of institutions and
other special establishments are shown in Tables LV, LVI, LVII and
LVIII. These include Asylums, Hospitals and other establishments for the
treatment and care of the sick and of persons mentally or physically
deficient, Prisons, etc., and their population represent in the aggregate
2.8 per cent, of the total civilian population.
The institutions are individually identified and their populations are
given by sexes.
3. Monastic Orders.
Table LVIII shows the number of religious houses in Malta and Gozo
and the population inhabiting these Convents and Monasteries, distinguish-
ing between British Subjects and foreigners. The number of Monks,
Novices and Lay Brothers, which was 446 in 1921, increased by 60 to 506
in 1931 of which 17 were foreigners, as against 15 in 1921; and the number
of Professed Nuns, Novices and Lay Sisters, which was 747 in 1921, rose
to 940 in 1931, an increase of 193; the number of foreigners which was
161 in 1921, decreased by 22 to 139 in 1931.
4. Naval, Military and Air Force Personnel.
The non-civilian population, viz: Officers and other ranks or ratings
of the Royal Navy, of the Army and of the Royal Air Force, from which,
for Census purposes ,local troops are excluded, numbered 16,617 as set out
hereunder :
Royal Navy 14,080
Army ... ... ... ... ... 2,066
Royal Air Force ... ... ... ... 421
Royal Fleet Auxiliary Service 50

Schoolmistresses and 23 nursing sisters.

Navy and Air Force enumerated on land.

H. M’s Ships in harbour on Census Day.

Census Day.
5. Harbour.
The number of persons enumerated in the Malta Harbours was 13,430
whereof
13,215 belonged to the Royal Navy
50 to the Royal Fleet Auxiliary Service
165 to Merchant Shipping and Harbour Craft.
The 13,215 persons of the Royal Navy and the 50 persons of the Royal
Fleet Auxiliary Service were all non-Maltese. Of the 165 on board merchant
vessels 162 were non-Maltese and 3 were Maltese.


£ were 45 British Ships of War in harbour and a number
ot reserve minesweepers, and other small craft, 2 Maltese coastal steamers
and one foreign steamer, and two Maltese and 26 Italian sailing vessels.
In 1 921 the harbour population enumerated was 8,066.
6. Parliamentary Constituencies.
Table LXII shows, by Parliamentary Constituencies, the civilian
population in each Electoral Division and the number of registered voters
on t.ie 1 931 Register. The male and female population of the age of 21
years and over is also shown.

Table XXXVIII. Showing the number of persons born in the Urban, Suburban and Rural
Districts of Malta, in Gozo and Abroad ; and the Districts in which they were enumerated.
N.B. This table, read vertically shows the birth place of all persons enumerated in each District; and read horizontally, shows the
place of residence of all persons born in each particular District or Abroad.
Table XXXIX- Showing, out of 1,000 persons in each District of Malta or in Gozo, how
many were born in each District of Malta, in Gozo, and how many Abroad.
NR-This table can be read only vertically, thusout of 1.000 persons enumerated in the Urban Districts 806 were born in those
Districts, 82 in the Suburban Districts, 45 in the Rural Districts, 16 in Gozo and >1 Abioad.

how many were present in the District ot birth and in the other Districts.
